 "cells": [
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "# Connectivity"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "One thing that may be bothering you about SIR is the idea of a population where all the possible interactions between individuals take place. You don't meet everyonme in your city, town, or village like that on a regular basis: that isn't how societies work.\n",
    "\n",
    "Admittedly you get this sort of \"complete mixing\" in *some* scenarios. A school for young children (or, even better, a toddler's nursery) perhaps works like this as the children go out to play together and everyone mixes with everyone else. And this is why diseases spread so rapidly through nurseries: one sick child will infect all the others.\n",
    "\n",
    "This phenomenon can be used constructively, and when I was a child it was commonplace for parents to hold \"chickpox parties\" and bring all the neighbouring children together to play so they could be exposed &ndash; and then subsequently be immune from further infection, since chickenpox is quite well-modelled by SIR {ref}`[1] <note:chickenpox>`.\n",
    "\n",
    "But in other cases the assumption of complete mixing is going to break down. And since we know that even a small change in infectivity can have a dramatic effect on the progress of an epidemic, can we change SIR to accommodate the social realities in which there are substantially fewer connections between individuals that we might expect?\n",
    "\n",
    "There are a couple of ways we could answer this question.\n",
    "\n",
    "Let's return to the equation where all the intreresting stuff happens:\n",
    "\n",
    "$$\n",
    "    \\Delta I = p_{\\mathit{infect}} \\, SI - p_{remove} I\n",
    "$$\n",
    "\n",
    "The first term essentially takes all the possible contacts (all $S \\times I$ of them) and \"exposes\" them to the $p_{\\mathit{infect}}$ factor that decides what proportion of them are infected. If we want fewer contacts to be exposed, we can simply add another factor &ndash; let's call it $f$ for \"fraction exposed\" &ndash; that determines what proportion of the contacts are taken into consideration. This would lead os to re-write the above equation as:\n",
    "\n",
    "$$\n",
    "    \\Delta I = p_{\\mathit{infect}} \\, (f \\, SI) - p_{remove} I)\n",
    "$$\n",
    "\n",
    "If $f = 0$ then there are no potentially infectious contacts; if $f = 1$ then *all* contacts are potentially infectious and we recover our original equation; and for values of $f$ such that $0 \\lt f \\lt 1$ we choose the fraction of contacts we want to happen."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "# Networks"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "There is another, more interesting, approach that uses networks, and it's this approach that we'll now turn to.\n",
    "\n",
    "A *network* is simply a collection of *nodes* connected by *edges*. A road map is a good example of a network: the nodes are the road junctions, while the edges (which connect the junctions together) are the segments of roads. This is the reason we sometimes refer to the *road network*: it's a collection of (bits of) road that meet at various points.\n",
    "\n",
    "Another good example of a network &ndash; and closer to our current application &ndash; is a *social network*. In a social network the nodes are people and the edges are \"social links\" or \"social contacts\" between them. While we usually think of social networks as being online, like Facebook or Twitter, they idea works in the real world too: when you meet your family, go shopping, go to work, or indulge in any of the activities of daily life you add edges to your social network between yourself and the people you meet. Typically the number of people you'll have social contact with will be considerably less that the entire population.\n",
    "\n",
    "Do the same thing for everyone in the population, and you have a social network for that population. The nodes are the individuals; the edges are the potentially infection-carrying social contacts.\n"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "## Complete networks"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "Suppose that, on your travels, you *did* actually happen to come into contact with everyone in the population, so that your social network had edges connecting you to everyone else. And suppose that everyone else did the same, so that everyone was connected to everyone. This is still a social network &ndash; a very dense one to be sure, but perhaps like that of a crowd at a rugby match. It also matches the assumptions we made earlier about complete mixing. So if we  ran a simulation of diosease spreading over this network, we'd presumably expect to see the same results as we did before.\n",
    "\n",
    "Wouldn't we?\n",
    "\n",
    "Let's try it. First we need the same equtions as we had before."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": null,
   "metadata": {},
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "import numpy"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 4,
   "metadata": {},
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "def make_sir(beta, alpha):\n",
    "    # turn the equations into update functions\n",
    "    def dS(S, I, R):\n",
    "        return -beta * S * I\n",
    "    def dI(S, I, R):\n",
    "        return beta * S * I - alpha * I\n",
    "    def dR(S, I, R):\n",
    "        return alpha * I\n",
    "    \n",
    "    # return the three functions\n",
    "    return (dS, dI, dR)"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 5,
   "metadata": {},
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "def epidemic_sir(T, N, pInfected, pInfect, pRemove):\n",
    "    # create the equations for these parameters\n",
    "    (dS, dI, dR) = make_sir(pInfect, pRemove)\n",
    "    \n",
    "    # initial conditions\n",
    "    sss = [ N * (1.0 - pInfected) ]   # everyone is initially susceptible...\n",
    "    iss = [ N * pInfected ]           # ...except for this fraction...\n",
    "    rss = [ 0 ]                       # ...and no-one starts off removed\n",
    "\n",
    "    # push the initial conditions through the differential equations\n",
    "    for t in range(1, T):\n",
    "        ds = dS(sss[-1], iss[-1], rss[-1])\n",
    "        di = dI(sss[-1], iss[-1], rss[-1])\n",
    "        dr = dR(sss[-1], iss[-1], rss[-1])\n",
    "        sss.append(sss[-1] + ds)\n",
    "        iss.append(iss[-1] + di)\n",
    "        rss.append(rss[-1] + dr)\n",
    "        \n",
    "    # return the time series\n",
    "    return (list(range(0, T)), sss, iss, rss)"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "And the same parameters."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 6,
   "metadata": {},
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "N = 2000\n",
    "T = 5000\n",
    "pInfected = 0.01\n",
    "pRemove = 0.001"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "We then need to simulate the epidemic spreading over the network. Fortunately Python has two libraries available to help us with this problem. The ``networkx`` library lets us represent networks in Python programs, which the ``epydemic`` library provides the tools for simulating epidemic (and other) processes running over those network."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": null,
   "metadata": {},
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "import networkx\n",
    "import epydemic"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "``epydemic`` has an SIR process built-in. Since we're interested in then progress of the epidemic we'll add some monitoring to get the same time series that we got for the continuous case. "
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 7,
   "metadata": {},
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "class MonitoredSIR(epydemic.SIR, epydemic.Monitor):\n",
    "    '''A monitored epidemic.'''''\n",
    " \n",
    "    def __init__(self):\n",
    "        super(MonitoredSIR, self).__init__()\n",
    "        \n",
    "    def build(self, params):\n",
    "        '''Build the observation process.\n",
    "        \n",
    "        :param params: the experimental parameters'''\n",
    "        super(MonitoredSIR, self).build(params)\n",
    "\n",
    "        # also monitor other compartments\n",
    "        self.trackNodesInCompartment(epydemic.SIR.SUSCEPTIBLE)\n",
    "        self.trackNodesInCompartment(epydemic.SIR.REMOVED)"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "We can now write a function to perform the simulation for given parameters of `pInfected`, `pInfect`, and `pRemove`. For completeness we'll also pass the function a network over which to run the simulation. The function sets up the simulation using ``epydemic``, runs it, and extracts the results."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 8,
   "metadata": {},
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "def network_sir(T, g, pInfected, pInfect, pRemove):\n",
    "    # create the simulator\n",
    "    m = MonitoredSIR()\n",
    "    m.setMaximumTime(T)\n",
    "    e = epydemic.StochasticDynamics(m, g)\n",
    "\n",
    "    # set the simulation parameters\n",
    "    param = dict()\n",
    "    param[epydemic.SIR.P_INFECTED] = pInfected\n",
    "    param[epydemic.SIR.P_INFECT] = pInfect\n",
    "    param[epydemic.SIR.P_REMOVE] = pRemove\n",
    "    param[epydemic.Monitor.DELTA] = T / 50          # take 50 samples\n",
    "    \n",
    "    # run the simulation\n",
    "    rc = e.set(param).run()\n",
    "    \n",
    "    # extract the time series\n",
    "    results = e.experimentalResults()[MonitoredSIR.TIMESERIES]\n",
    "    ts = results[MonitoredSIR.OBSERVATIONS]\n",
    "    sss = results[epydemic.SIR.SUSCEPTIBLE]\n",
    "    iss = results[epydemic.SIR.INFECTED]\n",
    "    rss = results[epydemic.SIR.REMOVED]\n",
    "\n",
    "    # return the time series\n",
    "    return(ts, sss, iss, rss)"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "To test our hypothesis, we need a complete \"social network\" to operate over. Such networks are often called *complete graphs*, and ``networkx`` can build one for us at whatever size we need, with every node connected by an edge to every other."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 30,
   "metadata": {},
   "outputs": [],
   "source": [
    "g = networkx.complete_graph(N)"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "Finally we can run the equations *and* the network simulation and plot them together. If they generate the same results, we'd expect the two sets of lines to agree with each other."
   ]
  },

    "pInfect = 0.000005      # chosen simply for illustration\n",
    "\n",
    "# run the epidemic equations\n",
    "(ts, sss, iss, rss) = epidemic_sir(T, N, pInfected, pInfect, pRemove)\n",
    "ax.plot(ts, sss, 'r-', label='suceptible (theory)')\n",
    "ax.plot(ts, iss, 'g-', label='infected (theory)')\n",
    "#ax.plot(ts, rss, 'k-', label='removed (theory)')\n",
    "\n",
    "# run the corresponding simulation\n",
    "(sim_ts, sim_sss, sim_iss, sim_rss) = network_sir(T, g, pInfected, pInfect, pRemove)\n",
    "ax.plot(sim_ts, sim_sss, 'rx', label='suceptible (simulation)')\n",
    "ax.plot(sim_ts, sim_iss, 'gx', label='infected (simulation)')\n",
    "#ax.plot(sim_ts, sim_rss, 'kx', label='removed(simulation)')\n",
    "\n",
    "# fine-tune the figure\n",
    "plt.title('Equations vs network ($p_{\\\\mathit{Infect}} = ' + '{b:.7f}$, '.format(b=pInfect) + '$p_{remove} = ' + '{a}$)'.format(a=pRemove))\n",
    "ax.set_xlabel('$t$')\n",
    "ax.set_xlim([0, T])\n",
    "ax.set_ylabel('population that is...')\n",
    "ax.set_ylim([0, N])\n",
    "ax.legend(loc='center right')\n",
    "plt.savefig('network-same-beta-alpha.png')\n",
    "plt.show()"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "And so they do: the crosses of the simulation lie over the top of the solid lines of the SIR equations."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "## Towards sparser social networks"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "But that's not a \"reasonable\" social network, any more than complete mixing was a reasonable assumption. A \"reasonable\" social network would have each person only meeting a few people &ndash; potentially a *lot* fewer. Meeting less people translates as having fewer potentially infection-spreading social contacts, so we might expect that the number of people the disease infects would drop as the social network of the population it was infecting grew sparser.\n",
    "\n",
    "What does \"sparser\" mean mathematically? In the complete social network, everyone was connected to everyone else. If the network has 2000 people, then you have 1999 edges: one less than the population, because you don't connect to yourself. We call the other people to whom you're connected your *neighbours*. The number of neighbours you have is called your *degree*. In this case, your node in network has degree 1999.\n",
    "\n",
    "If we look at everyone in the network, then *everyone* has the same degree: they're all neighbours with everyone (except themselves). If we choose a node at random, it will have degree 1999."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 41,
   "metadata": {},
   "outputs": [
    {
     "data": {
      "text/plain": [
       "1999"
      ]
     },
     "execution_count": 41,
     "metadata": {},
     "output_type": "execute_result"
    }
   ],
   "source": [
    "randomNode = int(N * numpy.random.random())\n",
    "len(list(g.neighbors(randomNode)))"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "If we were to look at *all* the nodes and take their *average* or *mean* degree, then this would be the same, since all the nodes have the same degree."
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "code",
   "execution_count": 17,
   "metadata": {},
   "outputs": [
    {
     "data": {
      "text/plain": [
       "1999"
      ]
     },
     "execution_count": 17,
     "metadata": {},
     "output_type": "execute_result"
    }
   ],
   "source": [
    "int(numpy.mean(list(dict(g.degree()).values())))"
   ]
  },
  {
   "cell_type": "markdown",
   "metadata": {},
   "source": [
    "The degree of a node is usually denoted $k$, for no particular reason; the mean degree is then denoted $\\langle k \\rangle$ &ndash; $k$ between angle brackets. $\\langle k \\rangle$ is a statistical measure of how \"dense\" the network is: a higher $\\langle k \\rangle$ means that nodes have on average more connections to other nodes. As $\\langle k \\rangle$ goes up the network gets denser and denser until it hits the maximum value where every node is connected to every other &ndash; we could denote this $\\langle k \\rangle_{max}$.\n",
    "\n",
    "We can now ask ourselves the question: what happens as we vary $\\langle k \\rangle$? We already know that when $\\langle k \\rangle = \\langle k \\rangle_{max}$ the network behaves like the equations. But what happens as $\\langle k \\rangle$ gets lower &ndash; that is, people have fewer and fewer contacts with each other &ndash; for the *same* disease? We'd presumably expact that fewer people will become infected overall, which also suggests that at some point the disease will simply die out without affecting very many people at all.\n",
    "\n",
    "Let's see."
   ]
  },
  {

   "execution_count": 42,
   "metadata": {},

   "source": [
    "(fig, axs) = plt.subplots(2, 2, sharex=True, sharey=True, figsize=(8, 6))\n",
    "\n",
    "pInfect = 0.000005      # chosen simply for illustration\n",
    "\n",
    "# draw sub-plots for the different values of kmean \n",
    "for (kmean, ax) in [ (1999, axs[0][0]),\n",
    "                     (1000, axs[0][1]),\n",
    "                     (500,  axs[1][0]),\n",
    "                     (100,  axs[1][1]) ]:\n",
    "    # create the random network with the given mean degree\n",
    "    g = networkx.erdos_renyi_graph(N, (kmean + 0.0) / N)\n",
    "    \n",
    "    # run the corresponding simulation\n",
    "    (sim_ts, sim_sss, sim_iss, sim_rss) = network_sir(T, g, pInfected, pInfect, pRemove)\n",
    "    ax.plot(sim_ts, sim_sss, 'rx', label='suceptible')\n",
    "    ax.plot(sim_ts, sim_iss, 'gx', label='infected')\n",
    "    ax.set_title('$\\\\langle k \\\\rangle = {k}$'.format(k=kmean))\n",
    "    ax.set_xlim([0, T])\n",
    "    ax.set_ylim([0, N])\n",
    "        \n",
    "# fine-tune the figure\n",
    "plt.suptitle('Random networks with different $\\\\langle k \\\\rangle$ ($p_{\\\\mathit{Infect}} = ' + '{b:.7f}'.format(b=pInfect) + ', p_{remove} = ' + '{a}$)'.format(a=pRemove))\n",
    "for i in [0, 1]:\n",
    "    axs[i][0].set_ylabel('population that is...')\n",
    "for i in[0, 1]:\n",
    "    axs[1][i].set_xlabel('$t$')\n",
    "axs[0][0].legend(loc='center right')\n",
    "plt.savefig('network-kmean.png')\n",
    "plt.show()"
   ]

   "source": [
    "The top-left graph corresponds to the graph above, and the disease progresses as we might expect. But as we reduce $\\langle k \\rangle$ we see a change in the behaviour: the epidemic gets \"thinner\", fewer people become infected overall and it takes longer to hit the peak of infections. And eventually the \"epidemic\" fails to take hold entirely."
